TUG1	None known	Characteristics	Multiple Tug1 isoforms, with a main 6.7kb transcript in mouse. Spliced (Young (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15797018).
TUG1	None known	Expression	Expressed in the developing retina and brain (Young (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15797018).. Expressed in adult tissues with highest expression in cortex (Young (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15797018). . Expression levels are elevated in the caudate nucleus of patients suffering from the trinucleotide repeat neurodegenerative condition, Huntington's disease (Johnson (2012) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22202438).. Localises to both the nucleus and cytoplasm (Khalil (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19571010). . Transcript was classified as unstable with a half-life >4 hr in human Hela cells (Tani (2012) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22369889). Similar results were found in human B cells and mouse 3T3 cells (Friedel (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19561200, Clark (2012) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22406755).
TUG1	None known	Function	Required for the proper formation of photoreceptors in the developing rodent retina (Young (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15797018).. Downregulation in developing retina leads to decreased retinal transcription factor expression and increased apoptosis (Young (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15797018).. TUG1 expression appears to be activated by p53 upon DNA damage, (Guttman (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19182780) with many p53 binding sites in the Tug1 promoter (Khalil (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19571010). Tug1 associates with PRC2 (polycomb repressive complex 2) and represses a number of cell cycle genes (Khalil (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19571010).
TUG1	None known	Conservation	Mammals - identified in mouse, rat, cow, dog and human (Young (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15797018).. Present in two locations in rat, with one an apparent processed pseudogene.
TUG1	None known	Misc	Nb: Mouse NRED accession is specific to one of two Tug1 isoforms.
TUG1	None known	Name	TUG1: taurine up-regulated gene 1 (Young (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15797018).
UCA1	CUDR	Characteristics	Spliced and polyadenylated with 3 exons (Wang (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18501714). . Multiple isoforms (1.4kb, 2.2kb and 2.7kb). The 2.2kb isoform contains an extended final exon compared to the 1.4kb isoform while the 2.7kb isoform has not been characterised (Wang (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16914571, Tsang (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17416635, Wang (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18501714).. Sequence is mainly repeat elements. Initiates in an LTR, exons sample pieces of a HERV-H element and longer 2.2kb isoform contains a second LTR at the 3' end (Wang (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16914571).
UCA1	CUDR	Expression	Widely expressed in embryonic development (Wang (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18501714) and placenta (Wang (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16914571).. Low or no expression in normal adult tissue but highly upregulated in cell lines resistant to drug induced apoptotic cell death and bladder, colon and lung cancer (Wang (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16914571, Tsang (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17416635).. Identified as a biomarker for bladder cancer (Wang (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16914571).
UCA1	CUDR	Function	Functions of 1.4kb and 2.2kb isoforms have been investigated, both appear to promote and enhance tumourigenesis (Tsang (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17416635, Wang (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18501714).. Over-expression of 1.4kb isoform enhanced tumorigenic behavior of bladder cancer cells in-vitro and in-vivo. Increased drug resistance and cell motility (Wang (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18501714).. Overexpression of 2.2kb isoform caused resistance to drug induced apoptosis, potentially by downregulation of caspase 3 levels (Tsang (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17416635).. 2.2kb isoform promoted transformation in-vitro, possibly because resistance to apoptosis is an important step in tumourigenesis (Tsang (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17416635).
UCA1	CUDR	Conservation	Found in humans. May be present in other primates but not in more distantly related mammals (Wang (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18501714).
UCA1	CUDR	Name	Cancer up-regulated drug resistant (CUDR). Urothelial cancer associated 1 (UCA1)
UM9-5	NCRNA00099, KCNIP4-IT1	Characteristics	Found in an intron of KCNIP4 in the sense direction.. ~2.3kb clones were identified in pig and human. NCRNA00099 which contains UM9-5 is ~10kb. Given it's position in an intron the full length of the transcript is somewhat unclear, cloning gave shorter transcripts, while transcription could be detected over whole NCRNA00099 site (but not outside it) in human.. Transcript contains a number of repeats. 
UM9-5	NCRNA00099, KCNIP4-IT1	Expression	Identified as a RNA that is more highly expressed in adult brain than fetal brain.. Expression in human and pig was similar. Mostly restricted to adult brain, highest expression in cerebellum.. Low expression was also detected in porcine embryonic tissues
UM9-5	NCRNA00099, KCNIP4-IT1	Conservation	Transcripts identified in pig and human. Southern blotting also could identify a homologous sequence in cow but not in rodents.
UM9-5	NCRNA00099, KCNIP4-IT1	Misc	KCNIP4 is a voltage-gated potassium (Kv) channel-interacting protein which is expressed in brain.. UM9-5 accessions: AY072607, AY072608, AY072609, AY072610 and AY072611
VAI and VAII RNAs	VA RNA I and VA RNA II.	Name	VA I and II: virus-associated RNA I and II.
VAI and VAII RNAs	VA RNA I and VA RNA II.	Characteristics	Human adenovirus encoded.. Transcribed by RNA polymerase III. Genes are separated in the adenovirus genome by a spacer of about 98 nt.. Highly structured RNAs of 160nt (VAI) and 163nt (VAII), with heterogeneous 3' ends and containing tightly structured stems.
VAI and VAII RNAs	VA RNA I and VA RNA II.	Expression	Cytoplasmic localisation.. Expressed at very high levels during viral replication. VAII is expressed at 20-fold lower levels compared to that of VAI.
VAI and VAII RNAs	VA RNA I and VA RNA II.	Function	Important role for viral replication in infected cells (upon VAI deletion, adenovirus replication is impaired in most cells). . Thought to block PKR activity, avoiding phosphorylation of eIF-2alpha and inhibition of viral mRNA translation (Thimmappaya (1982) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/6297772). PKR-binding secondary structure domain can adopt different conformations, which are functionally non-equivalent and exist in a mixed population (Wahid (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19635730). . Recently, it has been shown that VA1 has an important role in suppressing the RNAi response in infected cells during viral replication (Andersson (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16014917, McKenna (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16580685, Sano (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16472808, Xu (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17652395). Processed by Dicer into small RNAs that are incorporated into RISC (RNA-induced silencing complex). Approximately 80% of Ago2-containing RISC immunopurified from late-infected cells are associated with VA RNA-derived small RNAs (mivaRNAs), processed from the 3' strand of the terminal stems of the VA RNAs (Xu (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17652395). Nevertheless, VAII seems to be the preferred substrate for Dicer, accounting for approximately 60% of all small RNAs loaded in RISC (Xu (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17652395).. A deletion study shows that VAI may perform the role of bi-functional RNA by being processed by Dicer at the terminal stem to saturate the RNAi system, while generating a shortened VAI RNA that remains active to inhibit PKR. An RNA mutant that lacked the entire terminal stem was still able to bind PKR and inhibit its activity (Wahid (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18430723).. A fraction of the VAII-derived mivaRNAs are associated with polyribosomes, indicating a possible role in regulating translation of cellular mRNAs (Xu (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17652395).
VAI and VAII RNAs	VA RNA I and VA RNA II.	Conservation	N/A
VAI and VAII RNAs	VA RNA I and VA RNA II.	Misc	[[Similar mechanism of action of EBER1 and 2 encoded by the Epstein-Barr virus (McKenna (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16580685), and other viral or endogenous lncRNAs.]]
VegT RNA	None known	Characteristics	VegT is a bifunctional gene with functional RNA and protein products (Kloc (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16000384).. VegT protein is a T box transcription factor (Zhang (1996) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/9012531).
VegT RNA	None known	Expression	VegT RNA localises to the vegetal pole cytoskeleton in Xenopus oocytes (Zhang (1996) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/9012531, Kloc (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16000384).
VegT RNA	None known	Function	VegT RNA is necessary for the structure and integrity of the vegetal pole cytoskeleton (Kloc (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16000384) as well as the proper anchoring and localization of germ plasm islands and yolk platelets to the vegetal pole (Kloc (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17376434).  . Degradation of VegT led to the disintegration of the cytokeratin cytoskeleton (Kloc (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16000384).. Suggested to be integrated into the cytoskeleton holding subunits together or promoting subunit assembly (Kloc (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16000384).
VL30 RNAs	None known	Characteristics	Derived from VL30 mouse virus-like retrotransposons (Keshet (1980) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/6248245).. Transcripts of various sizes are detected by Northern blot, including a major band of ~5kb, but also bands of ~9 and ~11kb, depending on the tissue analysed (Harrigan (1989) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/2552295, Keshet (1980) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/6248245). 
VL30 RNAs	None known	Expression	VL30 RNAs are found ubiquitously in adult mice tissues (Harrigan (1989) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/2552295). VL30-1 RNA (a member of the VL30 family) is expressed 5- to 8-fold higher in mouse tumor lines than in mouse fibroblast or myoblast lines (Wang (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19805375), and is induced by pituitary hormones in steroidogenic cells (Song (2004) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/14704271).. Identified in the developing mouse periventricular germinal zones. It is also expressed extensively in the developing mouse CNS. Enriched in undifferentiated neurospheres derived from cortex in bFGF (basic fibroblast growth factor) and TGFa (transforming growth factor alpha), as well as from striatum in bFGF, compared to differentiated cortical neurospheres derived in bFGF. Also found expressed in both Embryonic Stem (ES) cells and Hematopoietic Stem Cells (HSC) (Easterday (2003) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/14651920).
VL30 RNAs	None known	Function	VL30-1 binds and regulates the PSF (PTB-associated splicing factor) protein, which contains 2 RNA-binding domains (RBDs), forming a PSF-RNA complex (Wang (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19805375).. VL30 RNA regulates steroidogenesis, and possibly other physiological processes, by forming a complex with PSF in mice (Song (2004) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/14704271).. Ectopic expression of VL30-1 RNA in a human tumor cell promoted metastasis (Song (2002) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/11959915), and in mice over-expression of VL30-1 RNA promoted cell proliferation in culture and tumorigenesis in-vivo (Wang (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19805375).. VL30-1 RNA is tumor-promoting whereas PSF is a tumor-suppressor agent, and the molar ratio of PSF protein and VL30-1 RNA regulates proto-oncogene transcription, cell proliferation, and tumorigenesis. VL30-1 RNA binds and releases PSF from a proto-oncogene and activates transcription of target genes (Song (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16079199). For example, in mice VL30-1 RNA has been shown to bind and release PSF from the proto-oncogene Rab23, activating its transcription in-trans (Wang (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19805375). (Although VL30 RNAs are not present in the human genome, a similar regulatory mechanism functions in human cells, in which different RNA fragments bind PSF (Li (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19625619).). VL30 is enriched in multiple stem cell populations and may have roles in determining  "stemness", or stem cell function (Easterday (2003) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/14651920).
VL30 RNAs	None known	Misc	VL30 mouse virus-like retro-elements comprise a family of sequences, 5 to 6 kb in length and containing long terminal repeats, with 100 to 200 copies dispersed throughout the genome (Keshet (1982) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/6287016).
WT1-AS	WIT1	Characteristics	0.4 - 3kb, spliced and present in multiple splicing isoforms (Dallosso (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17940140), as well as a protein coding isoform (WIT1) transcribed from a downstream promoter (Eccles (1994) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/8208551,Campbell (1994) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/8290269). Transcribed antisense to WT1 from intron 1 or from the same promoter region (bidirectional) of WT1, corresponding to a differentially methylated regulatory region (Eccles (1994) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/8208551,Malik (1995) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/7478583,Malik (2000) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/10811108).
WT1-AS	WIT1	Expression	Imprinted in humans (but not in mouse), with monoallelic (paternal) expression in normal kidney (Malik (2000) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/10811108,Dallosso (2004) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/14681303,Dallosso (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17940140).. Co-regulation with WT1 during mouse kidney development, and co-expression in mouse and human tissues, including fetal spleen, fetal kidney, and normal kidney  (Dallosso (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17940140).. Abnormal splicing of WT1-AS has been reported in acute myeloid leukaemia (AML) samples (Dallosso (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17940140).
WT1-AS	WIT1	Function	WT1-AS transcripts are exported to the cytoplasm and form heteroduplexes with WT1 mRNA, with a  proposed regulatory role in WT1 expression via RNA:RNA interactions (Dallosso (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17940140).
WT1-AS	WIT1	Conservation	WT1 has a positionally conserved antisense/bidirectional transcripts in mouse and humans, although WT1-AS  RNAs do not show extensive sequence conservation between these species, except at their 5' ends, which may extend into WT1 exon 1 (Dallosso (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17940140).
WT1-AS	WIT1	Name	WT1-AS: WT1 (Wilms' tumor 1) antisense transcript
Xist	None known	Characteristics	Spliced, polyadenylated, ~17kb in mice, ~19kb in humans.. Contains repeated sequence motifs A-F that are critical for its function.. Xist is located on the X chromosome in the X-inactivation center (Xic), an approximately 100-kb region in the mouse containing (as of June 2012) five other lncRNAs, many of which play crucial roles in X-chromosome inactivation (XCI). . Xist expression initiates the process of XCI in mammals.
Xist	None known	Expression	Xist is expressed in all somatic cells in female eutherian mammals, monoallelically from the inactive X-chromosome (Xi) (Brown (1991) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/1985261). Xist is not expressed in males. Xist is a nuclear RNA that coats the Xi (Brown (1992) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/1423611, Clemson (1996) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/8636206). . In the mouse, Xist expression is imprinted at specific times and places in development: Xist is transcribed exclusively from the paternally-inherited X (and thus the paternal X is always the Xi) only in the early pre-implantation blastocyst, and later in development only in extra-embryonic tissues such as the placenta.. Imprinted XCI in humans has not been demonstrated and may not exist (Moreira de Mello (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20532033).. In somatic tissues in mouse and human, XCI is random, not imprinted: either the paternal or maternal X can express Xist and become the Xi. This decision is made in each cell of the epiblast in the mouse, and the same X is inactivated in subsequent generations of daughter cells. Thus expression of Xist (and of nearly all X-linked genes) is mosaic, in the sense that each of the two alleles is active in different populations of cells.. Stage specific expression in different regions of developing mouse retina, with Tsix displaying a similar but weaker profile (Blackshaw (2004) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15226823).. In female undifferentiated mouse ES (embryonic stem) and iPS (induced pluripotent stem) cells, Xist is not expressed and both X chromosomes are active. Differentiation of mouse ES cells results in the onset of Xist expression and XCI, and thus these cells are an important model system for the study of XCI.. In contrast, Xist expression and XCI are variable in female undifferentiated human ES cells (Shen (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18339804, Silva (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18339803, Hall (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18340642, Lengner (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20471072) and iPS cells (Bruck (2011) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/21276761, Hanna (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19898493, Marchetto (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/21074045, Tchieu (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20727844), which may reflect variability in the broader epigenetic states and developmental potential of these cells.
Xist	None known	Function	Xist is the master regulator of mammalian XCI, the mechanism of dosage compensation that balances X-linked gene expression between males and females.. Xist function is required to initiate XCI (Penny (1996) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/8538762, Marahrens (1997) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/9009199). Ectopic Xist expression from an autosomal transgene causes long-range gene repression in cis (Wutz (2000) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/10882105).. Xist and RepA (see associated components below) RNAs bind to and recruit PRC2, which catalyzes the deposition of the repressive chromatin mark H3K27me3 on the Xi (Zhao (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18974356, Kaneko (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/21123648, Kanhere (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20542000, Zhao (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/21172659).. Small RNAs formed from Xist/Tsix duplexes may play a role in regulating Xist expression and XCI (Ogawa (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18535243). However, loss of Dicer has only a modest effect on Xist expression (Kanellopoulou (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19164542), contrary to what would be expected if RNAi is a major regulator of XCI.. hnRNP U (also known as SP120 or SAF-A) binds Xist through its RGG domain while the SAF domain is also required for localisation to the Xi. hnRNP U is required for both Xist localisation to the Xi and the initation of X-inactivation (Hasegawa (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20833368.. Xist Repeat C sequences are required for Xist localization to the Xi (Beletskii (2001) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/11481485, Sarma (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/21135235).. YY1 can interact with several DNA binding sites within exon 1 of Xist, and also to the repeat C (and perhaps repeat B) RNA sequences of the Xist transcript. Thus YY1 tethers Xist RNAs to the Xi and nucleates the Xist cloud that coats the Xi (Jeon (2011) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/21729784).. Xist forms a transcriptionally silenced sub-nuclear domain (Chaumeil (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16912274) and perinucleolar localisation of the silent Xist coated chromosome has been observed (Zhang (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17512404). The A region repeats are essential for the recruitment of late inactivated X genes to the silencing compartment and for the recruitment of the silencing complex PRC2 (Maenner (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20052282).. After the initiation of XCI, Xist function is not absolutely required to maintain XCI in fibroblasts (Csankovszki (1999) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/10431231). Nor is continued Xist expression required to maintain XCI-like repression around an autosomal Xist transgene in male, differentiated ES cells (Wutz (2000) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/10882105). However, lack of Xist function in differentiated cells can lead to partial reactivation of select X-linked genes (Csankovszki (2001) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/11352938).
Xist	None known	Conservation	Xist is present in all eutherian (placental) mammals examined to date, but it is not present in marsupials or monotremes (Duret (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16778056, Davidow (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17333538, Hore (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17333539, Shevchenko (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17333537). An unrelated ncRNA in marsupials, Rsx, is likely to play a role similar to that of Xist in XCI (Grant (2012) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22722828).. The most conserved Xist RNA region, the A region, contains eight or nine repeats separated by U-rich spacers.
Xist	None known	Acknowledgement	Thanks to Derek Lessing - http://molbio.mgh.harvard.edu/labs-user.php?userid=183 and other members of the Lee lab - http://molbio.mgh.harvard.edu/leeweb/index.htm for writing the majority of this entry.
Xist	None known	Name	Xist: X inactive-specific transcript
Xlsirts	None known	Characteristics	Family of lncRNAs. Contain varying numbers of ~80bp tandem repeats flanked by unique sequence (Spohr (1981) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/6173488). . Xlsirts localisation mediated by a 137bp repeat thought to fold into multiple stem loops (Allen (2003) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/12919101). 
Xlsirts	None known	Expression	Localises to the vegetal pole cytoskeleton in Xenopus oocytes. (Kloc (1993) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/7505061, Kloc (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16000384).
Xlsirts	None known	Function	Necessary for the structure and integrity of the vegetal pole cytoskeleton (Kloc (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16000384) as well as the proper anchoring and localization of germ plasm islands and yolk platelets to the vegetal pole (Kloc (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17376434). . Degradation of Xlsirts caused the 3D structure of the cytokeratin cytoskeleton to collapse (Kloc (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16000384). . Suggested to be integrated into the cytoskeleton holding subunits together or promoting subunit assembly (Kloc (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16000384).
Xlsirts	None known	Name	Xenopus laevis short interspersed repeat transcripts
Y RNAs	YRNA, RNY RNAs, hY RNA (human)	Characteristics	83-112 nt. Four human Y RNAs (hY1, 112nt; hY3, 101nt; hY4, 93nt; and hY5, 83nt) encoded in single copy genes, which are present in a cluster in human chromosome 7 (Maraia (1994) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/7520568). Pyrimidine-rich, transcribed by RNA polymerase III, and contain a 5-triphosphate and a retained 3' poly(U) tail (Hendrick (1981) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/6180298).. Y RNAs form characteristic stem-loop structures, with a highly conserved double-stranded stem formed by partially complementary 5' and 3' terminal domains, linked by a loop domain containing secondary stem-loops (Teunissen (2000) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/10606662, O'Brien (1993) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/7688474, van Gelder (1994) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/8041611). Parts of the RNA structure appear to be flexible, able to adopt different conformations. Y RNAs contain an essential characteristic bulged C in the middle portion of the stem  (in position 8 or 9), as well as the characteristic pyrimidine-rich region that is single-stranded in the secondary structures (Teunissen (2000) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/10606662, Green (1998) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/9671049).
Y RNAs	YRNA, RNY RNAs, hY RNA (human)	Expression	All four Y RNAs are primarily cytoplasmic, they are also detected in perinucleolar compartments, discrete structures near the nucleolar rim (Matera (1995) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/7539809).. Y RNAs are expressed in similar levels in all human tissues and cell lines investigated, with all Y RNAs showing a positively correlated expression across different tissue types (Christov (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18283318)(Pruijn (1993) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/7505620). Y RNAs are up-regulated in a number of human solid tumours compared to corresponding nonmalignant tissues. In particular, hY1 and hY3 RNAs are over-expressed in carcinomas and adenocarcinomas of the bladder, cervix, colon, kidney, lung and prostate (Christov (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18283318).
Y RNAs	YRNA, RNY RNAs, hY RNA (human)	Function	The different Y RNAs may have multiple roles.. Y RNAs were identified as the RNA component of the 'small cytoplasmic' (sc) Ro autoantigen (soluble  ribonucleoproteins (RNP) detected in sera of systemic lupus erythematosis patients), binding the autoantigen La and Ro proteins (Lerner (1981) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/6164096).. Y RNAs have been shown to be required for semiconservative chromosomal DNA replication in vitro, in a mammalian cell-free experimental system. hY1, hY3, hY4 and hY5 RNAs are functionally redundant (Christov (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16943439), and have been shown to act at the initiation step of DNA replication* (Krude (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19657016). (*See Krude (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20012579 for review).. Regulate cell DNA replication and cell proliferation, with knock-down of hY1 and hY3 RNA in human cells causing a cytostatic arrest of cell proliferation (Christov (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18283318, Krude (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19657016, Christov (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16943439).. Bind to Ro proteins through their terminal stem, which contains conserved binding sites for Ro60 and La proteins (Green (1998) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/9671049). The central portion of the double-stranded stem is essential for reconstituting chromosomal DNA replication, while the central loop and the terminal stem are not (Gardiner (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19474146).. Different proteins may also bind Y RNAs or be present in human Ro RNPs, such as hnRNP K, PTB, RoBP1 and nucleolin (Bouffard (2000) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/10668799, Fabini (2000) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/10785401, Fouraux (2002) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/12096904), indicating that Y RNAs may be involved in their regulation, such as in the control of alternative splicing. hY5 RNA uniquely binds to ribosomal protein L5 and its binding partner 5S RNA, with Ro-Y5 suggested to have a specialized role in 5S ribosomal RNA quality control (Hogg (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18056422). Ro60 protein had been previously shown to bind misfolded small RNAs and suggested to be involved in quality control of the RNAs (Shi (1996) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/8752087, Stein (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15907467, Fuchs (2006) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17041599). A role is also proposed in the regulation of the translation of ribosomal mRNA (Pellizzoni (1998) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/9710533).
Y RNAs	YRNA, RNY RNAs, hY RNA (human)	Conservation	A single cluster of functional Y RNA genes is preserved throughout tetrapod evolution, which however exhibits clade-specific tandem duplications, gene-losses, and rearrangements (Mosig (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18087752).. Homologues of functional Y RNAs are present throughout vertebrates, with conserved synteny, but numbers vary between species, with individual gene-loss events having occurred during evolution in most classes (the mouse expresses only Y1 and Y3 RNAs, zebrafish only Y1, and chicken Y3 and Y4). No Y RNA gene has been identified in some species such as Drosophila melanogaster and Schistosoma species. (Copeland (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19814823, Pruijn (1993) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/7505620, Mosig (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18087752, Gardiner (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19474146, O'Brien (1993) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/7688474, Perreault (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17470436, Farris (1995) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/7534247). Y3 is the most conserved Ro RNA, not only by its more consistent presence in other species, but also at the levels of sequence divergence and secondary structure similarity (Farris (1995) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/7534247). . Recently, the 'sbRNAs' or stem-bulge RNAs from Caenorhabditis elegans have been identified as homologs of vertebrate Y RNAs present in nematode worms, showing the characteristic Ro protein binding motif (Boria (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20349053).. An Y-like RNA (as well as other small RNAs) has been also identified in the bacterium Deinococcus radiodurans binding to an ortholog of Ro60 (Rsr), which accumulate following UV irradiation, and contribute to the resistance to irradiation (Chen (2000) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/10766734).
Y RNAs	YRNA, RNY RNAs, hY RNA (human)	Misc	Thousands of Y RNA 'retropseudogenes' are present among various species (Perreault (2007) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17470436). It has been suggested that hY RNAs represent a novel class of nonautonomous, L1-dependent, retrotransposable elements (Perreault (2005) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15817567).
Zeb2NAT	ZEB2AS	Characteristics	>1.2 kb; antisense to Zeb2 (a transcriptional repressor of E-cadherin) (Nelles (2003) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/13679026).
Zeb2NAT	ZEB2AS	Expression	Expression is upregulated by Snail1 (Beltran (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18347095).
Zeb2NAT	ZEB2AS	Function	Controls translation and therefore function of the Zeb2 protein coding gene through regulation of Zeb2 mRNA splicing (Beltran (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18347095).. Zeb2 translation requires retention of an intron, Zeb2NAT overlaps the 5' splicing site of this intron and when expressed inhibits splicing allowing translation of Zeb2 (Beltran (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18347095).. Snail1 acts to stimulate Zeb2 synthesis via post-transcriptional regulation which appears to be mediated by Zeb2NAT (Beltran (2008) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18347095).
Zeb2NAT	ZEB2AS	Conservation	Good conservation between human and mouse (Nelles (2003) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/13679026).
Zeb2NAT	ZEB2AS	Name	Zeb2NAT: Zeb2 natural antisense transcript
Zfas1	Znfx1 antisense, 1500012F01Rik, NCRNA00275	Characteristics	Expressed from a bi-directional promoter with the protein-coding gene Znfx1. Zfas1 5â is antisense to 5â of Znfx1.. Spliced, polyadenylated. . SnoRNA host gene, contains 3 intronic C/D box snoRNAs.. Human orthologue is alternatively spliced. 
Zfas1	Znfx1 antisense, 1500012F01Rik, NCRNA00275	Expression	Highly expressed and differentially expressed during mammary development. Strongly down-regulated during lactation compared to pregnancy and then up-regulated again during involution. Znfx1 coding gene,in contrast, was more lowly expressed and was not differentially expressed during mammary gland development.. Expressed in the epithelial cells of the mammary gland ducts and alveoli.. Zfas1 is widely expressed in mouse tissues including brain, liver, lung, kidney, spleen, heart and embryo, showing higher expression than Znfx1.. Zfas1 extremely stable with a half life of >32 hrs in N2A (neuroblastoma) cells. Compared to a half life of 50 minutes for Znfx1. The increased stability of Zfas1 may explain why its expression level is much higher than Znfx1. However, in mouse 3T3 and embryonic stem cells Zfas1 stability was much lower with a half-life of 1.7 hr and 63 min respectively, while the human ZFAS1 had a half-life of 3 hr in B-cells (Friedel (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19561200, Sharova (2009) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19001483, Clark (2012) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22406755). These results suggest there is significant regulation of Zfas1 stability both within and between species (Clark (2012) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22406755).. Localised to both the nucleus and the cytoplasm. . Human ZFAS1 expression is reduced in ductal carinoma compared to normal tissue.
Zfas1	Znfx1 antisense, 1500012F01Rik, NCRNA00275	Function	Zfas1 appears to have a role in mammary gland proliferation and differentiation.. - Zfas1 knockdown in a HC11 cell culture model of mammary differentiation showed increased differentiation and a probable increase in proliferation. Knockdown had little effect on intronic snoRNA expression suggesting the Zfas1 host transcript is functional.. - Zfas1 knockdown appears to affect Znfx1 expression.
Zfas1	Znfx1 antisense, 1500012F01Rik, NCRNA00275	Conservation	Syntenic transcription observed in multiple mammals.. Low conservation of Zfas1 sequence between human and mouse compared to snoRNAs, but Zfas1 has potential secondary structural similarities.
Zfas1	Znfx1 antisense, 1500012F01Rik, NCRNA00275	Name	Zinc finger antisense 1
Zfhx2as	Zfh-5AS	Characteristics	Antisense transcription begins 6-10kb downstream of the Zfhx2 polyA site and ends in first exon of Zfhx2.. Alternatively spliced. Multiple start sites.. Estimated as up to ~30 kb in length.
Zfhx2as	Zfh-5AS	Expression	Expressed in a number of specific regions in the developing mouse brain including the rostral migratory stream (RMS). . Antisense RNA was expressed in a complementary manner to the sense coding sequence. For example the antisense RNA was expressed initially in hippocampal development, with the sense RNA only starting to be expressed when antisense transcription declined. Or, alternatively in the dorsal forebrain where sense and antisense transcripts are expressed in different layers of cells at the same stage.. Expression was highest in mid-gestation E13.5-E16.5.. Adult expression specific to brain, with expression in areas of adult neurogenesis.
Zfhx2as	Zfh-5AS	Function	Zfhx2as negatively regulates the expression of Zfhx2.. Knockout of antisense RNA lead to up-regulation of Zfhx2 expression, with coding gene expression in areas previously showing only expression of the antisense transcript. The same effect was seen in a mutant where Zfhx2 and Zfhx2as no longer overlapped strongly suggesting Zfhx2as negatively regulates Zfhx2.
Zfhx2as	Zfh-5AS	Conservation	Identified in rodents and humans.
Zfhx2as	Zfh-5AS	Misc	NCBI Gene and Refseq sequence given for Zfhx2as (provided here) is only a partial sequence from the center of the antisense transcript. . Since full length transcript not defined, UCSC browser links are best estimates of transcripts. Mouse transcript comes from a Scripture transcript defined by Guttman (2010) - http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20436462.
Zfhx2as	Zfh-5AS	Name	Zfhx2as: zinc finger homeobox 2, antisense
